0316212377|5.0|Like children growing up, People and intelligent machines evolve their way through millennia towards Sublimation, transference of their minds and bodies to a higher order universe. Some, like the citizens of the Culture, remain in our universe despite being sufficiently advanced to Sublime. Others, like the Gzilt, tumble headlong towards this next evolution. The Gzilt are only a few days away from Subliming, but a disturbing disclosure from the Zidhren - one of the Gzilt's mentor cultures - threatens their future. In the end it's down to a four-armed musician with just the right chance acquaintance and the curiosity and interference of the Culture to preserve the Gzilt's chosen future.<br /><br />It's hard to believe this is the tenth Culture novel: it still feels fresh to me. Banks' writing is beautiful, witty and engaging, pulling the willing reader into the Culture universe and keeping them there. This book has everything I love about the Culture: well-meaning Culture interference, the god-like Culture ships, and reflection upon myriad aspects of life. What does it mean to grow up? How do you make the decision to Sublime to a world which you've heard is wonderful, but which you have nothing but words to prove it? When have you lived too long, and what does it require mentally?<br /><br />I found the characters endearing, and the story engaging and interesting. Even to the end I had ways I hoped it would work out, but it could have gone either way. There is the standard share of villains and heroes, each with their own motives and justifications and darkness.<br /><br />This is a good Culture book. Though not quite reaching the pinnacle I felt with [[ASIN:0316005401 The Player of Games (Culture)]], this is easily in the top three of the series, and the top ten books I've read in the last ten years.<br /><br />If you're a sci-fi fan and haven't experienced the Culture, I strongly recommend reading this book, and then perhaps Player of Games, and some of the others. Although the universe is self-consistent, each book stands alone, and they can be read in any order.<br /><br />Well done, Mr. Banks! 5 stars, as usual where your writing is concerned :)Although the universe is self-consistent, each book stands alone, and they can be read in any order.<br /><br />Well done, Mr. Banks! 5 stars, as usual where your writing is concerned :)
B0047S1KMS|5.0|Odds are you hadn't heard of this clever horror gem. DEAD SET is a British television mini-series that documents an instance of zombie apocalypse. Running at approximately 141 minutes and broken up into five episodes, DEAD SET first aired on the E4 network from October 27 to 31, 2008. And it is riveting stuff. It is harrowing and darkly comic and, most importantly, doesn't condescend to its viewers. It merges zombies with reality television. It's another kick-in-the-arse commentary on conformity within our society and our fixation on pretend televised sensationalism. It's a wicked satire, brought about by exec. producer/writer Charlie Brooker's scathing wit.<br /><br />I'd never seen the Big Brother show. And, frankly, I despise those who fawn over them trumped-up celebrities. It's disturbing to me that in the UK, where it originated, Big Brother is even more popular. (I guess having a damn cool accent doesn't necessarily equate to sophistication.) DEAD SET tracks the cast and crew of the obnoxious Big Brother reality show, and for those who are in the dark as I am, its premise goes like this: Contestants selected for their inability to get along with each other are plonked in a confined space and each week are voted off the show by the viewers. As DEAD SET opens, the contestants and the production crew are so wrapped up with the shooting of the latest episode that news of a cataclysmic national emergency pass them by.<br /><br />I saw this mini-series with a cousin who is a reality show fiend (and, yes, I despise him for it). But he filled me in with some background material. Such as that the in-film presenter, Davina McCall, is the real-life UK Big Brother presenter. And that the UK version of the Big Brother house is a fortified compound on the Elstree studios lot. Wire fences lace the compound's perimeter. It is regularly patrolled by security to keep the public out. Apparently, the set designers of DEAD SET have reproduced the Big Brother house to an almost exact likeness. Meaning that those familiar with the show and the house's layout will get that extra thrill. But for the purposes of serving the zombie narrative, the compound and those barricaded within amp up the genre's often used siege element.<br /><br />The ensemble cast is uniformly solid, even the actors relegated to the stereotype roles (the hot bimbo, the creepy guy, the hunk, the street-savvy homie...). But the two standouts are Andy Nyman as the absolutely hateful producer \\"Patrick\\" (he gets the best dialogue) and Jaime Winstone as \\"Kelly,\\" the harassed production runner/gofer and eventual plucky heroine. Kelly provides the narrative thread. She's our point-of-view character. I appreciate that Kelly does the sensible thing even in the midst of blind panic. She retrieves her weapon (How many times have we seen someone absent-mindedly discard their weapon?). Before exiting a room, she peeks under the door to determine safe passage. It's gratifying to see someone who isn't prone to typical horror movie stupidity. I do question that Kelly and several others, in such short time, become such deadeye shooters.<br /><br />There's satisfaction for the gorehounds. DEAD SET may be a television mini-series but it doesn't stinge on gore and blood and brutal kills. It's got more-than-decent production values. And, yes, for viewers of gentler persuasion, there is profanity. Because \\"Yikes!\\" is not an appropriate expletive when you're being targeted by the undead. However, taking a panic dump in a bin IS very much appropriate.<br /><br />All five episodes are on one All-Region disc. Bonus stuff is as follows:<br /><br />From Episode One:<br />- \\"Davina McKill\\" - An interview with reality game show presenter Davina McCall while she has her zombie make-up put on, followed by a behind-the-scenes look at one of her zombie scenes (00:06:51 minutes)<br />- \\"SFX: You've Got to Get Them in the Head\\" - the f/x crew looks at the fire extinguisher as a zombie killing device (00:02:45)<br />- 2 Deleted/Extended Scenes<br /><br />From Episode Two:<br />- \\"Interview with director Yann Demange\\" (00:04:21)<br />- \\"On Set with DEAD SET - interviews with the cast (00:06:13)<br /><br />From Episode Three:<br />- \\"Interview with exec. producer/writer Charlie Brooker\\" and if you can't guess whom Patrick the Producer is based on after this interview, well... 0143122843|5.0|I think I'm becoming a major Philip Kerr fan. This eighth novel in the historical series featuring the very world-weary Bernie Gunther, private eye and anti-Nazi extraordinaire, is perhaps the best of the bunch. Bernie's been drafted back into the cops, working as a detective in Berlin in late 1941. He's been to the \\"east\\" as an SS officer (after Heydrich drafted him, leaving him no other options) and he's glad he's back in Berlin, but he's grumbling about the conduct of homicide investigations, the way things are done and so forth. When Heydrich interrupts the latest case to insist that Bernie come south to Prague, where he's essentially the colonial governor of what's now the Czech Republic, Bernie follows orders and goes, bringing his girlfriend along. Heydrich announces he needs Bernie to act as a bodyguard, and figure out who's trying to kill him...and soon after, one of Heydrich's aides is murdered, and Bernie must solve that, too.<br /><br />The book whirls along at an impressive pace, and works pretty well. I have one weird quibble with the way it's written: I don't see the need for a chapter of 130+ pages in length, when some others are 2-3 pages long. Chapters are supposed to break the book up into manageable parts, so that you have a place to stop; at 130+ pages, no one's going to read that in one setting (or not many people, anyway). Aside from that, it's a very interesting book, well-done and amusing. It also brings Reinhardt Heydrich to everyone's attention again. For those who aren't familiar with him, he was the guy Hitler supposedly wanted to succeed him, a truly devoted Nazi who enthusiastically fought as a Nazi reservist in the Luftwaffe even though he didn't have to. His plane actually got shot down in Russia. On the negative side, it was Heydrich who chaired the conference at Wannsee where the Nazis decided what to do with the \\"Jewish problem\\" as they termed it, the conferees of course deciding to go with death camps, as opposed to trying to send them somewhere remote like Madagascar. Heydrich was a truly evil character, but a true believer and a brilliant administrator and leader. He also had a J. Edgar-like intelligence apparatus that gathered intelligence on other Nazis, through wiretaps, phone taps, and intercepted mail, and so he was much more powerful than he would at first appear. Given what an evil snake he was, he deserves to be forgotten, but prudence dictates otherwise...

Their journey is that of a nation in turmoil, reverberating clear into today.<br /><br />But this book is so quintessentially of America you can't separate the two, and all the better if you are reading this book with some intimate knowledge of the US. Even if you don't know America, this is a great exposure to the dramatic juxtaposition between rich and poor that has defined us all the way up until today. Chapter 5, where Steinbeck outlines the role of banks in the plight of the Okies, could easily have been written about 2009-era America and its ravaging by Bear Sterns, Lehman Brothers and Goldman Sachs - some 80 years later.<br /><br />The scene painted by the characters in this book evokes rich imagery, from the listless Al, to the frightened Rose of Sharron, the transcendental preacher Jim Casy and the practical, short-tempered Tom. 0307352153|5.0|Quiet is well written and well researched and offers a wealth of information about the differences between introverts and extroverts. Extroverts prefer action to contemplation, risk taking to certainty, and they are comfortable with conflict. They also make fast decisions, multitask, think out loud, and are talkative and interesting.<br /><br />Introverts, in contrast like being alone, enjoy being cooperative, work slowly and deliberately, have great power of concentration, think before they speak, express themselves better in writing than in speaking, dislike conflict. They are apt to be sensitive and serious.<br /><br />Cain makes a clear case for the fact that extroverts are highly valued in our culture and introverts are ignored or even looked down on. She explains in detail the valuable contributions introverts make to our culture and our world. They are the thinkers, creative and visionary. This is an excellent, valuable book.<br /><br />I was surprised Cain did not cite The Introvert Advantage: How to Thrive in an Extrovert World. Though quite different from each other, both are excellent books on a much neglected subject. The Introvert Advantage offers more practical help for navigating life in an extroverted world. For a full picture, read both books.

B00BCZU8WM|5.0|Eight years ago Justin Timberlake released his second studio album [[ASIN:B000H305U0 FutureSex / LoveSounds]],an album very much defined by uptempo funk and EDM musical ideas and hybrids. The grooves were emphasized over the vocals a lot of the times, and it was quite a creative departure from his debut. In the time since that release Justin Timberlake has taken time to star in feature films and,for awhile seemed to be joining the ranks of Elvis Presley and Whitney Houston who traded in their musical banner for shots at the silver screen. But not only did his theatrical roles turn out to be a big success, but they seemed to have inspired him creatively as well. Most popularly inclined music artists today have gone backwards to a degree to emphasizing single songs again. Mainly,as with the 45 RPM record half a century ago, that format is more in tune with the age of internet based formats such as YouTube and MP3's. Not only is Timberlake a fan of dressing and grooming himself in a sharp,elegant manner but his time in film probably exposed him more to the medium's dynamic way of presenting its vision. So since Timberlake bought \\"sexy\\" back last time out,he is now bringing the musical quality of the album format back in a similar fashion.<br /><br />  The album opens with the upbeat melody and stop and start groove of \\"Pusher Love Girl\\",with Timberlake's sensuously subtle vocal approaching wrapping around the hiccuping rhythms as he compares a strong relationship to an addiction. Hearing it here I've warmed up heavily to \\"Suit & Tie\\",a percussively  rhythmic funk piece where Jay Z's rap is perfectly in tune with the musical setting and never intrudes on Timberlake's melodic vocal harmonies. \\"Don't Hold The Wall\\" blends a slower 2-step dance groove with a trance music-type harmonic atmosphere,complete with East Indian singing and flutes. \\"Strawberry Bubblegum\\" is one of my favorite songs here. It begins with a lightly pulsing,spare electro funk and builds in it's last three minutes into a afrolatin dance/funk/percussive jam. The enormous rhythmic dynamics of \\"Tunnel Vision\\" and the slower \\"Spaceship Coupe\\" but more of the emphasis on Timberlake's songwriting and vocals again-as he vocally harmonizes with himself Marvin Gaye side to express both the romantic and carnal side of his personality in these songs. \\"That Girl\\" is a sweetly melodic neo soul type number-led by a fantastic jazzy guitar riff that again places the focus on his singing and melodicism. \\"Let The Groove In\\" is just amazing-a hyper kinetic funk era style jam that is heavy on a genuine African percussion/tribal dance rhythm.<br /><br />  \\"Mirrors\\" is a very potent mixture of modern soul and progressive pop/rock that grows stronger in tone as the song progresses-especially in terms of its melodic development. The final song \\"Blue Ocean Floor\\"-with it's spare trance/electronica sound and backwards loops takes the overall approach of this entire album to its most basic level. And what is the overall approach of this album exactly? In a modern production concept from Justin and Timbaland,who produced the previous album, Justin Timberlake has managed to bring out a re-visitation of the cinematic psychedelic soul/funk/pop-rock sound that permeated music by Isaac Hayes and much of the Norman Whitfield era Temptations. The songs here are generally seven + minutes and therefore have enough space to develop instrumentally and vocally. But as opposed to relying on a backup vocalists and enormous orchestral instrumental passages with no vocals, Timberlake's own talents in singing/songwriting are integrally linked to the conceptually dynamic instrumental approach this album takes. And the mixture of tradition string and horn arrangements with modern day EDM/hip-hop electronics give this album the possibility of being the commercial fruition of how music in the last three or four years has began to grow out of the unsettling complacency its been in since the turn of the millennium. Considering this albums already strong commercial success, if an approach to music like this catches on in the coming years, the funk/soul album innovations of the century might reach their commercial golden age.m. Considering this albums already strong commercial success, if an approach to music like this catches on in the coming years, the funk/soul album innovations of the century might reach their commercial golden age.

B002MUAJ2A|3.0|I am a near-original Rails developer, and a guy who loves the attitude and approach that Fried and DHH (Hanson) promote in their books.  But this one takes their ideal past a limit of utility.<br /><br />Here's an analogy: go to any Rails API page and check out the code.  It's brilliant.  It's awesome.  It's terse and simple.  It's elegant.  But because of all of these things, the top-level APIs of Rails all call second-level APIs, which are also abstract, wonderful, cool, terse and simple.  And that's great -- this is &#34;how to write beautiful software&#34; in its essence.  But if you're actually trying to figure out how a given method works, you have to drill down into the methods called by the top-level.<br /><br />And this is the weakness of Rework.  It is short and sweet -- an easy read and direct to the meat, no doubt.  But, in the same way that you need to believe the top-level Rails methods work, you are asked to believe that the top-level methods described in this book work, too.  My experience says that both Rails methods, and those proposed in Rework both work ... but while you can test the former, there's a great leap of faith required to accept the latter.<br /><br />Frankly, I think the book is thin -- not just the absence of fluff, but the absence of demonstrable, clear evidence that their approach towards business is applicable to yours.  In the end, the ideas are all sound, in my experience, but they devolve to just assertions of truth, rather than demonstrations thereof.  I think I want more.

B000E1ZBGS|5.0|This is a magnificent production. The cinematography is pure art and can compete with the great paintings of the past: I think I have never seen the like of it. Some deviations are made from the book, all seemingly in order to obtain those stunning images - Darcy's proposal for instance is made under a solemn, late 18th century garden \\"temple\\"; in the pouring rain, rather than prosaically in the small cottage guest room she occupies at Lady de Bourgh's; the denouement is even more torn out of any convention, but it yields the strongly symbolic mage of two lovers in grassland embracing amidst haze and dew against the rising morning sun: I buy it.<br />I cannot vouch for the story's consistency because I know both the book and the superb 1995 BBC series too well to be able to imagine learning the story by way of this motion picture. Other than that, the acting is superb; Keira Knightly (with a name like that, when is she going to do Emma???) magnificently portrays both the serious and frivolous sides of an intelligent and wise 22 year old; Matthew Macfadyen had to grow on me (and who in the world can top Colin Firth's plunge in the pond of his castle?) but convinces; Donald Sutherland, though in my view a miscast, is his superb self; and Judy Dench places a most formidable and implacable Lady Catherine. Everyone else is thoroughly enjoyable.<br />The music is well done, fortunately with period players (the English Concert): what a chance, nowadays, for a film director to depict musicians the way they played in the time of the story! This period playing is combined with 21st century music depicting the characters' emotions, and all is amalgamated into a good whole. The costumes are very well done too, maybe a bit too upscale (difference is made though between the rich and the less so), but they blend better into the stunningly gorgeous pictures than (perhaps) simpler, more realistic clothing would.<br />One of the most beautiful movies I have ever seen.<br />I wonder if Keira's blowing out a candle somewhere in the middle of the movie is a small wink at Jennifer Ehle in the BBC production, who does the same, in pretty much the same way.

B00C5ZORES|5.0|Not even two months ago, Fall Out Boy was still split up - at least to everyone's knowledge - and the band's future was uncertain. And now, we have their fifth album ready to download. As a massive fan of the band, it feels almost surreal. It begs another question as well: how good can an album that was recorded suddenly and in absolute secrecy be? As it turns out, very good. Fall Out Boy, unhindered by expectations from fans, made an album that's quite different from all of their pre-hiatus material, yet still feels true to themselves. This is not a sell-out album, as some might have you believe. Some detailed song-by-song observations are below, though for those of you who just want the quick run-down, here are some concise thoughts:<br />* Fall Out Boy is NOT a pop-punk band anymore. Sorry to anyone who had an investment in that side of the band. I would say that they most resemble pop-rock now. I'd say this new stylistic shift has allowed them to create arguably their best album yet... however, some may be inclined to disagree. Please know going in, however, that this is NOT a pop-punk album and does not attempt to be.<br />* This album is supremely catchy. Almost any song on here could probably go on the radio and have some degree of success. In that sense, this album's reminiscent of fun.'s latest record, \\"Some Nights.\\" Again, this is not a bad thing-- it's just different.<br />* Patrick sounds better than ever before. If you thought he was good on \\"Folie\\" or \\"Soul Punk\\" (his solo record), you'll be even more blown away by his progression here.<br />* From a parent's standpoint, this album is both a bit edgier and a bit cleaner that Fall Out Boy's pre-hiatus material. It's edgier in that there are more profanities. \\"Where Did the Party Go\\" and \\"Save Rock and Roll\\" both feature the f-word, and one swear shows up on \\"Young Volcanoes\\" as well. In addition, Courtney Love and Big Sean deliver heavily-profanity-laden guest spots on \\"Rat A Tat\\" and \\"The Mighty Fall,\\"two otherwise clean songs. However, this album is also a bit more clean than Fall Out Boy's latest, in that it feature far less sensuality than usual - the band seems to have matured, focusing on other topics besides just romancing girls (although there's still a decent amount of that). This section may not relate to most people, but parents wondering whether or not the album is safe to play in front of the kids may need to assess their ability to handle harsh language.<br />* Yes, it's different, but it's not as if Fall Out Boy suddenly sold out. This album is a natural progression of the direction they'd already started in \\"Infinity on High\\" and developed on \\"Folie a Deux.\\" If you like \\"Folie,\\" you'll probably like this album. If you hated \\"Folie,\\" well, this album's probably going to be even worse. I think \\"Folie\\" may be their best album, so I was inclined to like this release.<br />* Buy this album. It's fantastic, and there's not a single bad song on the album. It's different, but give it a few listens and you'll get used to it and like it.<br /><br />SONG-BY-SONG:<br />THE PHOENIX - Kicks off with a bang. Massive, epic, and dramatic, it's everything that their first track back as a band should be. Patrick gives a standout vocal performance. I'm not a huge fan on the overly synth-y chorus, which seems just a smidge TOO dancey, but the rest of the song is hard to find any fault with. 4.5/5.<br />MY SONGS KNOW WHAT YOU DID IN THE DARK (LIGHT EM UP) - The only incredibly long song title on this album, sadly (another thing that Fall Out Boy's changed). But man, what a song it is. The synth loop at the beginning might scare you into thinking this'll be overly poppy, but it's probably one of the darkest, rockiest songs on the record. Patrick's \\"I'm on fire\\" is wonderfully unexpected and feels like a classic rock anthem. 4.5/5.<br />ALONE TOGETHER - What would happen if Fall Out Boy covered Maroon 5? This is probably pretty close to the answer to that question. I'd call this one of the poppiest songs on the record, and for some of you, that'll be an instant turn-off. It's catchy as all get-out, and I'll be incredibly surprised if it's not a radio single at some point (after \\"My Songs\\" and \\"Young Volcanoes\\"). It really grows on you with repeated listens. I like it now. 4/5.<br />WHERE DID THE PARTY GO? - I'd say this is arguably the worst song on the album, and it's not even BAD. It's just... not at the level of the rest of the songs. A little catchy, but nothing too memorable, and it feels overly synthy. The needless profanity on the second verse kind of subtracted from it for me, too, though that's a personal thing. 3/5.<br />JUST ONE YESTERDAY - Patrick feels practically blues-y from the opening lines, and the songwriting is vintage Fall Out Boy - a little nonsensical, but super-clever and witty at the same time. This song feels like it could have been ripped from \\"Folie a Deux.\\" Only suffers due to being a bit unmemorable in the context of the album. 4/5.<br />THE MIGHTY FALL - I'm not a fan of Big Sean. He didn't add anything to the song except the need for a radio edit. That being said - this song is fantastic despite him. The chorus is massive and will get stuck in your head all day, and Patrick's as commanding as ever in the vocals. It's very reminiscent of \\"My Songs,\\" so your thoughts on that song will probably apply here. 4.5/5.<br />MISS MISSING YOU - This one's a grower. I didn't care much for it, or even remember it, upon my first listen. But this song has a way of getting into your head the more you listen to the album. \\"Baby, you were my picket fence\\" is going to kill in a concert setting. It's designed for that sort of arena-style sing-along, and really embodies the new sound that Fall Out Boy is embracing on this record. 4/5.<br />DEATH VALLEY - Another very-\\"Folie\\"-ish song. Fans of the band's older work will probably find the most to like on this song. I've heard someone compare the song to Queen, which doesn't ring entirely true to me, although I can see where they're coming from. It's a pretty great song, although it kind of suffers from blending in among the other, catchier songs. 4/5.<br />YOUNG VOLCANOES - Boldly going where Fall Out Boy has never gone before, and on a mission towards complete and total radio domination. Seriously, it's just a matter of WHEN this song utterly takes over radio for months. The song is pure pop and catchy, and fans will be totally caught off-guard by the song. Give it a try, and you might just be won over by it. 4.5/5.<br />RAT A TAT - I don't know what the heck Courtney Love is doing here. The song would have been much stronger without her, as was the case with Big Sean. That being said, this song is probably the closest Fall Out Boy gets to returning to their bratty, pop-punk roots. The music is really fun and danceable, but without the synth issues that \\"The Phoenix\\" had. The gang vocals take some getting used to, but it gets better with repeat listens. 4.5/5.<br />SAVE ROCK AND ROLL - An absolute beast of a finale. Fall Out Boy's best finale yet, and probably the spiritual successor to \\"What a Catch, Donnie\\", but better. I'd be surprised if anyone hated this song, even old fans who miss pop-punk Fall Out Boy. The emotion and energy on this song is transcendent. It's an anthem, in the truest sense of the word, and one that won't leave your head for days. Elton John becomes the first guest singer who actually really adds to the song, as he and Patrick's vocals complement each other nicely. I can't lavish enough superlatives on this song. 5/5.<br /><br />So that's \\"Save Rock & Roll\\". People are going to make fun of the title and say that they're not rock anymore, but that's not even remotely true. Fall Out Boy has always been poppy, to some degree. That's implied by the \\"pop\\" in \\"pop-punk.\\" \\"Sugar, We're Going Down\\" and \\"Dance, Dance\\" were both poppy singles, albeit in different ways than this album. This album stakes out new territory, but doesn't sell out in the process. The songs are great and not just easy, churned-out radio hits. You owe it to the band to at least give this album a listen, and to make up your mind for yourself, beyond the hype. You might just like it. I know I did.

0393061310|1.0|Over the past few years, I have read books that I consider superfluous.  I will critique them.<br />Ideology Trumps Fact<br /> I am a great admirer of Jared Diamond.  He obviously has such wondrous elitist status, as how could I not?  He is a scientist in physiology but nevertheless this book under review is what I would call comparative archaeo-anthropology.<br /> The premise:  It had been noticed that while human beings are supposed to be 98% the same biologically (as claimed by science, nor do I doubt it), the conditions of various nations and peoples are vastly unlike.  Some have been very successful, the Europeans and Eastern Asians, others have not, mainly in Africa but also in Australia and in other areas thought to be remote; these have been calamitous failures.  Or, to put it in specific terms:  Nigeria, because of its geographical size, its large population and its natural resources, should be a successful country and part of the G-20 nations and make a G-21.  But it is not.  The last I heard about Nigeria was that parts of its former capital and the commercial and cultural hub, Lagos, were returning to jungle.  The same could be said for South Africa.  A reason for this state of the planet wanted to be found by certain elements of American society for ideological concerns.  They wanted to find a cause other than \\"intelligence,\\" attributed to Europeans and certain East Asians, for the rise of nations and peoples.<br /> Therefore, Diamond wrote his book.  First of all, while Diamond is a scientist in his field, this book is a popularization of subjects that are not his field --- and even though an erudite popularization is one in error.  The book fails to prove what Diamond set out to do.  He admits it.  Further, almost every assertion about peoples and the planet could be challenged by an expert in the field involved.<br /> The belated societies, further, have something that Eurasians did not have when they started: They have the example of the successfulcountries themselves, in their history, political struggles, technology, medical and even weapon advances and even literature.  These the dominant peoples would give them, and have - and either country to country or by individuals.  Nor has this worked, except potentially in a small way in some countries.<br /> Diamond does not prove his thesis, and the book is not partially a failure; it is totally a failure.  It is stuffed with \\"ifs\\" about the development of the planet and its peoples as far back as the Pleistocene. Nevertheless, the author has gotten kudos throughout the academic and publishing fields for his attempt to prove that something other than intelligence is involved in the rise of peoples and nations.  I did not want a \\"good try.\\"  I wanted a book that established other reasons for the welfare of nations than intelligence, and the book was presented that way.  The subtitle says \\"The Fates of Human Societies,\\" which is an absolute statement, as if it were an established fact, or a serious hypothesis under peer review.  Rather, the book is near totally conditional and contingent; or, to speak plainly, false.<br /> Further, the book quickly got boring to me, as I perceived that it was neither science nor fiction --- but a vast collection of facts in specious organization.  (Perhaps it might fall into the genre of \\"creative nonfiction\\" that means, rather, \\"arrant nonsense.\\")<br /> I do not know what the reasons are for the perceived and persistent differences between peoples.  Some such biogeographical considerations as Diamond posits may have meaning as part of an answer, if one is ever established.  But Jared Diamond has not presented the solution.  He posits a vast array of interesting facts and developments but we are still as ignorant of the reasons for the developments of human societies as we were before his book.  The supposition that geography and climate affect human societies is not new.<br /> But then, in another millennium, the world might change in a way that will allow the gifts of the belated peoples to thrive and dominate.  (We should remember that Europeans, about CE 1000, did not appear to be on their way to dominate the planet, if we can forget Byzantium --- that we have.)<br /> Nor do I disbelieve that New Guineans may be, on average, more intelligent in their milieu than Westerners/Americans are in theirs.  (This is especially true with American politicians who must be the dumbest creatures on the planet, whatever sub-species they are.)<br /> Nor, if they do not want to construct a modern, industrial, technological, profit-driven, exploitive society in their own lands, that could be a fact in their favor.<br /><br /> Therefore, since there was no answer to the question of the peoples of the planet in their various degrees of --- Success?  Dominance?  Effectiveness in the world? --- the book is superfluous and a waste of my time to read and thus irritates me.  The author and publishers might be accused of falseness in advertising.  The project of the book might even be termed propaganda.

0156034026|4.0|The Reluctant Fundamentalist by Mohsin Hamid tells the story of a Pakistani citizen named Changez, his move from an undergraduate degree at Princeton to an NYC-based valuation firm called Underwood Samson & Company, and his relationship with the WASP-ish Erica, pre- and post-9/11.  Changez narrates the entire novel--at times in first person, but often in second person as well, as Hamid frames the story as a conversation between Changez (now returned to Pakistan) and an un-named American man in the Old Anarkali Bazaar in Lahore.  Changez invites the American man to try a cup of tea, which turns into a full meal and then dessert.  What begins as a seemingly harmless conversation slowly darkens as the novel progresses.<br /><br /> Just as Changez's politeness masks something perhaps more sinister, the novel's apparent stream-lined simplicity masks its depth and richness.  The novel resounds with the theme of isolation v.s. openness and appearances v.s. reality.  Obsessed with her memories of Chris, a childhood friend-turned-lover who has passed away, Erica retreats further and further into herself, preferring to live in her own fantasies rather than in the real world.  Her inward movement mirrors America's growing mistrust of \\"foreigners\\" and increasing isolationism post-9/11.  Changez, on the other hand, moves from an isolated focus on solely \\"getting the job done\\" and appraising companies to realizing that the reports he and his company makes affect the companies' employees--real people trying to make a living.  He begins as well to view himself as a perpetual outsider, and to connect these companies to larger flows of capital--capital that is disproportionately held by people of Erica's complexion rather than his own.<br /><br /> Another theme in the novel, connected to that of isolation and openness, is that of control.  Hamid's novel is very much a novel of \\"talking back\\" ( la Edward Said)--of insisting that one's voice be heard in a world that so frequently ignores or talks over it.  The only voice we truly hear in The Reluctant Fundamentalist is Changez's.  Changez holds his two layers of audience (the unnamed American and the reader holding the book) essentially captive.  While he includes dialogue between himself and other characters, one is always aware that this dialogue is filtered through Changez's limited first-person report of the conversation.  As such, Hamid also conflates the reader with the uncomfortable American, who, twitchy and suspicious, views everyone around him as a potential threat.<br /><br /> Interestingly, however, the novel does not entirely reassure us that the figures of whom the American is suspicious aren't, in fact, a threat to him.  The novel ends ambiguously.  Because we only receive the information Changez gives us, we will never know what actually happens on the dark road in front of the American's hotel.  In other words, the novel itself is dangerously uni-vocal, warning us through its very structure against only listening to one side of any story.<br /><br />  Hamid's novel thus utilizes a technique--oral storytelling--often explored in post-colonial novels, and with, I think, the intent of \\"talking back\\" against the dominant, hegemonic voices, and it explores post-colonial themes of fractured identity, diaspora, and flows of global capital.  But it does this so subtly, one might barely register the way it very faintly echoes sprawling epics like Patrick Chamoiseau's Texaco or Salman Rushdie's Midnight's Children.  Hamid's novel is like its narrator--intelligent, well-mannered, deeper than it may appear, focused, controlled, and sinister.  While giving a 3D portrayal of a (mostly) sympathetic man and telling what appears at first to be primarily a love story, it also raises questions that it doesn't fully answer--not just about the text itself, but also about how to live ethically, fully, and also safely as an American, as a Pakistani, and as a global citizen post-9/11.

B000E0W24S|5.0|It is mind-boggling to me that there is anyone out there who could consider this MANDATORY set of Tchaikovsky's last three symphonies, performed so magnificently by the great Yevgeny Mravinsky and \\"his orchestra\\" the Leningrad Philharmonic, as anything less than SPECTACULAR, THRILLING, DEEPLY MOVING, and DEFINITIVE.  This is one of the greatest recorded legacies in all of classical music, and essential listening.  Anyone who loves Tchaikovsky and does not hear these performances is cheating themselves out of one of the greatest musical experience imaginable.  My classical CD collection numbers well over 1,500 CDs, and I would EASILY put this set among the 10 greatest recordings I own.<br /><br />Even if you know these symphonies and have other performances that you are quite satisfied with, you simply must hear these, because I do believe these are the final word on the subject.  Mravinsky's interpretations of these works go right to the very core of these symphonies, with thrilling, over-the-top performances that make the jaw drop while being deeply moving at the same time.  The first time I heard the performances of these symphonies, I experienced both shock and euphoria, and felt like I was truly hearing this music for real for the first time.<br /><br />So what's so special?  There are a lot of appropriate adjectives here, but one in particular nails it for me:  these are *dangerous* performances.  Mravinsky pushes the music and the orchestra to the absolute limit in every way, and by all rights, both the music and the playing should fall apart, but they don't.  Instead, what emerges is so revelatory, so shocking in its beauty, intensity, passion, and even wildness.  There is a *truth* to these performances that makes you feel like this is exactly what Tchaikovsky intended to say, and wanted us to hear, that makes it difficult to imagine there is any other valid approach.<br /><br />Mravinsky's performances here of the 4th and 5th are revelatory and spectacular.  Yet best of all to my ears (if that's even imaginable) is the recording of the 6th.  Mravinsky's performance of the 6th is not simply as good as the others, but even more so, it sets off shockwaves.  There is such an intensity to this 6th that it's impossible for me to listen to it and not be moved to tears, every single time.  The pain is real and visceral, as are all the other emotions that Tchaikovsky conveys, including joy, sorrow, momentary triumph, and ultimate tragedy.<br /><br />Some criticize the playing of the Leningrad Philharmonic as rough and disorderly.  Maybe that's true at the margins, but to criticize these performances for such nit-picking reasons not only overlooks so much playing which is spectacular, but it is reductionist in a way that misses the forest through the trees.  And in the end, do the details really matter a whole lot when there is such music-making going on?  The music is so alive, the performances so involved, and everyone is playing as if their lives depend on it.  This is nothing less than great music-making in the finest sense, and when real music-making shows up, the details just don't matter.<br /><br />Some call these performances \\"histrionic.\\"  That's an arrogant, reductionist comment that indicates a lack of appreciation for what Mravinsky and the Leningraders actually achieve here, and I feel sorry for anyone that would have such a response.<br /><br />No one will mistake the sound for being 2013 state of the art, but you know, it's pretty wonderful sound overall, with richness, beautiful resonance, and an overall sound profile that perfectly fits the performances and actually adds to them, and in no way detracts from them.<br /><br />While any of the details can be criticized, the whole of these performances is so successful and so exceeds the sum of the parts that the \\"parts\\" not being perfect in whatever way simply doesn't matter.  These performances are the achievement of a lifetime, and even if Mravinsky never did anything else worthwhile (and that's far from the case), these performances truly achieve greatness.<br /><br />Quite simply, this is one of the greatest achievements in the entire history of recorded music.nything else worthwhile (and that's far from the case), these performances truly achieve greatness.<br /><br />Quite simply, this is one of the greatest achievements in the entire history of recorded music.

0439023521|1.0|This book was a total letdown and an unfortunate example of how hype can adversely affect independent thought.  There's just no way this book would have made the impact it did otherwise.<br /><br />I had reasonable expectations for The Hunger Games.  I wasn't excited to read it, but I'd heard enough good things that I at least expected to enjoy it.  Boy was I wrong. It was a total waste of time.<br /><br />First, the entire book is written in first person, passive present tense.  Example: \\"I'm sitting on a riverbank, tying my boot.\\"  Are we supposed to be in her head and she's narrating her life to us at that very moment?  It's awkward and limiting, and a big no-no when writing pretty much anything.  It was like reading a really bad screenplay. Collins' prose was also extremely dry.  There was no beautiful description to draw readers into the story like any good book should have.  It fell extraordinarily below the expectations of alternate reality fantasy.  She could have done so much more with the world she created.  In fact, the film went beyond what was in the book.  Example: Despite the Capitol possessing technology like hovercrafts, the Capitol train was a regular train on tracks.  In the film, they stepped it up and turn it into a hover train.  What, Collins couldn't think that up herself?<br /><br />Second, the characters were dull, one-dimensional and unsympathetic.  I didn't care what happened to them.  By the end of the book, I didn't care whether or not Katniss lived or died.  Don't mistake me--I don't believe that a protagonist has to be likable, but she at least needs to be one I can root for.  She was strictly utilitarian; just a plot device as opposed to a real person.  The characters in the film were inherently more sympathetic.  That should never happen. A movie script has an average of 120 pages with a lot of white space.  There's only so much time to work with.  But a novel allows an author considerably more freedom with backstories, description,exposition and character development.  The film should be a shadow of the book, not the other way around.<br /><br />Third, I expected some deeper meaning to the book as was expressed by friends and the author.  It wasn't there.  Either people were doing everything they could to associate the book with issues like war, totalitarianism and global warming (yep, that was a claim made by Collins herself) when it wasn't actually there, or Collins intended for those issues to be there but simply lacked the talent to mete our her desires and hoped to remedy that by claiming such associations during her book tours.  Really--I looked for those metaphors; I'm well-educated in governmental and political issues, but they just simply weren't there.  The only potential metaphor was life under a dictatorship. But any of the subtlety, the prose and exposition reflected few, if any, deeper political meanings.<br /><br />I can only conclude that the reason this book became so popular was because it was crowned the anti-Twilight; a story with a teenage, female protagonist that wasn't a love story.  It must have been quite liberating for the teen girl audience that had become accustomed to the shallow, obsessive love story that is the Twilight series. But just because you find an unbroken dish in a pile of shattered remnants, doesn't mean it belongs anywhere other than a second-hand store.

B008R5OKI8|5.0|Each of the Vivaldi Edition's (the Vivaldi Edition is a project to record all pieces found in the composer's personal archive in Turin) discs of Violin concertos have a theme. The theme of this disc is 'For Pisendel'. Pisendel was one of the great virtuosos of the first half of the 18th century. He studied with Vivaldi in 1716 and perhaps a year or so later. These lessons were informal, as Vivaldi seemed to view the German as a colleague. But Vivaldi did show Pisendel some things on the violin and gave him lessons in composition. Part of their friendship and personal relationship was that Pisendel had many Vivaldi scores dedicated for his use (per Pisendel) or he was allowed to copy works that interested him and bring them back to Germany. This recording has both dedicated works and the ones which were copied by Pisendel.<br /><br />The part of Pisendel on this disc is played by violinist Dmitry Sinkovsky. He has a fresh sound and a strong, even wild virtuosity. In an attempt to live up the the reputation of a famous virtuoso of the past so respected by Vivaldi, Sinkovsky adds small cadenzas of his own to many of the works. The orchestra, Il Pomo D'Oro sounds good here and blends somewhat better than on the L'Imperatore disc, although it could still compete for attention with the soloist more, tonally and stylistically. Over all, it is a great disc bringing together difficult, high-quality works and presenting an excellent Baroque violinist I've not heard before. Works Included are:<br /><br />Concerto in C RV 177. This work dates from after 1730, and is in Vivaldi's strange late style. This shows that Pisendel had an active interest in Vivaldi's music even at this late date, and that somehow they were in contact enough for him to receive scores. This performance is a less polished but fun addition to Carmignola's version, although here the 3rd movement tempo is taken too slowly.<br /><br />Concerto in D RV 212a. Vivaldi composed this work for himself to preform atthe 1712 feast of St. Anthony, and it includes several written cadenzas. Pisendel's interest in the pure virtuosity of the work is understandable, and it is possible that Vivaldi 'updated' the work for him. The 'a' version has a more modern and lyrical sonata-style slow movement, while the old version has a more recitative style slow movement (as does RV 208 from the same period). You could call RV 212 the Vivaldi version (see Adrian Chandler) and 212a the Pisendel version.<br /><br />Concerto in d minor RV 246. Pisendel seems to have shared Vivaldi's dramatic flair on the violin and had a fondness for minor keys.  This work has a dark, rugged feel and uses double stopping freely.<br /><br />Concerto in B flat RV 370. The liner notes state that the dating of this work is problematic, but estimates that it's from the first half of the 1720's. Adrian Chandler (who already recorded this piece) seems to think it dates from around 1716 (or before), when Pisendel was visiting Venice. I'm inclined the go along with this based on several factors. Either way, it is a showy, well written piece with a place to pause for a cadenza.<br /><br />Concerto in d minor RV 242. Even though this work dates from around 1716, it made Vivaldi's cut for Opus 8 (1725). Perhaps he was fond of it, or maybe that inclusion was a nod to Pisendel. The last movement is a study in double stopping, a technique which appealed greatly to Pisendel.<br /><br />Concerto in B flat RV 379. Based on the language, this work, published in Opus 12, is later than most associated with Pisendel. The feel of the work is classically Vivaldian, with aggressively joyful mood and rhythmic vitality. The opening solo starts with a  great hook.<br /><br />Concerto in g minor RV 328. There is a fiery side to the melancholy when Vivaldi writes in g minor. This piece is a perfect example, being dark and depressed, but containing moments of anger. It certainly is something that would appeal to Pisendel, who enjoyed introspection as a refuge from display.

0143038419|5.0|Reading \\"eat, pray, love\\", whilst one of the best sellers and most popular books, happened to stir in me many delights and yearnings. I loved it through and through.<br /><br />Elizabeth Gilbert paints a world of joy, bittersweet sorrow, and entertainment in her self-discovery. I find her writings overwhelmingly beautiful. It is not often that I can identify so much with the personality of an author and the yearnings of her search. I hardly have much in common with her situation: She is divorced, lives in NYC, and travels alone to 3 countries in one year of her life. I love her guts. I love that, as a woman, she can so boldly state that she did not wish to have children and that she was unhappy with a great man in her marriage and her fancy apartment in New York. I absolutely admire that she can claim, without any apology, that she cannot find happiness in her seemingly perfect life - oddly enough- a life which until then was her own picture of idealism.<br /><br />At the age of 34, Elizabeth Gilbert, in all her devotion to pursuit of a dream, decides to give up everything she has built in pursuit of the longing of her heart and a journey of self-discovery. She defies cultural notions that we should be happy if we are married to a good person and that the maternal instinct should kick in right around 30 for a healthy, normal woman. Finally, when her bouts of depression become intolerable, she makes the commitment to that self-discovery and sets off for her one year journey to three countries whose names start with an I - suiting her mission to self-actualization even more perfectly if you care to put significance into small coincidences - Italy, India, and Indonesia.<br /><br />Elizabeth's prose is unique and poetic; I was engrossed in her world. Her passages read like a symphony, music to my ears. At the time, I had constantly dreamt of visiting Italy and for lack of no good reason, it had still not happened. Elizabeth Gilbert gave me Italy better than any guidebook or travel journal. She writes with subtlety about her experiences and is herself surprised by the enormous joy she is reaping from it all - watching Italian boys play football in the streets,  choosing her gelato flavors carefully, thoroughly enjoying every bite of her food, taking Italian lessons, and engrossing in life as it unfolds in the form of daily routine for the average Italian.<br /><br />I admire the most about Elizabeth Gilbert her thirst for adventure; it leaves me green with envy. How she travels to a country so far and away to find a 100-plus year old medicine man she met years ago is an example of Elizabeth's care-free, risk-loving, it-will-all-work-out attitude, that to me contrasts with her constant worrisome traits. Off she goes to Bali, and through a luck-struck bizarre and ingenious way, she most certainly finds her medicine man and spends the last four months of her adventure in beautiful Bali.<br /><br />She is an ordinary novice at travel, without so much as preparing an itinerary or making a plan before getting on the road. She experiences horrible incidents of indigestion from the thought of travel alone and has hardly any tolerance for non-ordinary foods and unfamiliar climates. She is as equally unprepared to travel alone on such a self-discovery mission as a child. Yet she gets through it all by exploiting her one key strength: her ease in making friends with anyone, anyone at all.<br /><br />Our `novice' traveler sets on her journey to the countries of Italy, India and Indonesia, in that order. The order in which she visits her countries carries a significance that she shares like so: Italy is for indulging in life, food, sights, sounds, India is for indulging in health, purity, yoga, meditation, and reflection, and Indonesia is to return to the medicine man she met years earlier, without so much as having a name, address, or a plan to aid her search.  The uncertainty and lack of preparation thrills and worries her at the same time -it tests her tolerance for the unknown, and in a bizarre way, seems to cause her to mature and grow to not worry about small details. What is the worst possible scenario? Well, she may just not find her medicine man. Are we conditioned to worry and plan too much during times when the journey needs to take its own course? There is so much at stake if we do not plan - a waste of time, money, resources, a lost opportunity, a frustrating journey - and while all of that is plausible, the unknown still has a thrill and risk has a taste that fills us with envy and curiosity.<br /><br />Elizabeth Gilbert tells us poignantly about her struggles in the Ashram in India; these were among my most favorite passages of the book. The experience of Ashram by itself would provide difficult enough for anyone taking on a journey of self-discovery, but particularly for her so recently parting with her indolent, carefree days in Italy. The atmosphere and environment of Italy and India and the nature of her journey in each could not be more in contrast. While in the Ashram, she struggles to adjust. Sitting in meditation for hours. Waking up at the wee hours of the morning to sit in this silence for what seems like eternity. Doing yoga for a while before returning to silence and to more meditation. The discipline, the sacrifices of instant pleasures for the sake of a higher pursuit. These make up some of the core messages of \\"Eat Pray Love\\" by the brave and open spirited Elizabeth Gilbert.it tests her tolerance for the unknown, and in a bizarre way, seems to cause her to mature and grow to not worry about small details. What is the worst possible scenario? Well, she may just not find her medicine man. Are we conditioned to worry and plan too much during times when the journey needs to take its own course? There is so much at stake if we do not plan - a waste of time, money, resources, a lost opportunity, a frustrating journey - and while all of that is plausible, the unknown still has a thrill and risk has a taste that fills us with envy and curiosity.<br /><br />Elizabeth Gilbert tells us poignantly about her struggles in the Ashram in India; these were among my most favorite passages of the book. The experience of Ashram by itself would provide difficult enough for anyone taking on a journey of self-discovery, but particularly for her so recently parting with her indolent, carefree days in Italy. The atmosphere and environment of Italy and India and the nature of her journey in each could not be more in contrast. While in the Ashram, she struggles to adjust. Sitting in meditation for hours. Waking up at the wee hours of the morning to sit in this silence for what seems like eternity. Doing yoga for a while before returning to silence and to more meditation. The discipline, the sacrifices of instant pleasures for the sake of a higher pursuit. These make up some of the core messages of \\"Eat Pray Love\\" by the brave and open spirited Elizabeth Gilbert.

This was to emphasize her point, that her lack of awareness and consequential lack in that situation is much like those of us who struggle with need and desire, unaware of the energy field surrounding us, ready to respond if we will simply flip the switch.<br /><br />While I didn't need more proof than I already had of the validity of her claim, I have to admit to being pleasantly surprised by the extremely easy experiments and the quick responses. The author asks us to simply ask for a blessing with 48 hours as evidence of its reality.<br /><br />The next 48 hours brought several blessings to me, all could have been discarded as coincidences (if you believe in those) but the one that provided a slice of humorous, concrete evidence was the shim.<br /><br />Soon after starting the experiment a thought crossed my mind, \\"I need a shim\\". For those of you who aren't familiar with the term, it's commonly used in carpentry. According to the Free Dictionary, a shim is \\"a thin, often tapered piece of material, such as wood, stone, or metal, used to fill gaps, make something level, or adjust something to fit properly.\\" Since I'm not a carpenter it's not something that I make reference to very often, although I have a few scattered around my house.<br /><br />That afternoon I left to visit a friend in a neighboring city and planned to spend the weekend. They were more of a novelty item since they could be hung on a key chain. I very nearly fell out of my chair. I had not participated in more than the one single thought of needing a shim. B00BKBCF38|4.0|I had the good fortune of getting an early pressing of Ghost's sophomore release, so I've been digesting this album for the better part of a week now. Good thing, because as with Opus Eponymous, I'm finding that this is more of a grower than a shower. While a couple tracks struck me right out of the gate, the majority took time to make an impact. (And to be honest, I'll need a few more weeks before I have a fully-formed impression of the album as a whole.)<br /><br />In keeping with the medieval theme of Opus, the title track begins in reverb-soaked Latin plainchant. The band enters full force soon thereafter with an infernal fanfare celebrating the anti-Christ child's arrival. The album continues through the first few tracks at the same insistent pace, guitar riffs and snare drum front-and-center with the ubiquitous pipe organ creating an effectively sinister backdrop.<br /><br />Just fifteen minutes deep and \\"Ghuleh\\" takes the album for an unexpected turn. I'm not sure how I feel about the jarring change, not having delved too deeply into the lyrical content, but I can only assume it's meant to follow the path of the story. The tune itself came off as hokey to my ears at first, but I've grown to admire its vulnerability; there's something compelling in Papa's voice, something that quietly breathes life into the story. The driving tempo returns with \\"Zombie Queen,\\" which juxtaposes funereal organ against some unlikely Ventures-esque surf guitar passages.<br /><br />Chanting clerics are back for \\"Year Zero\\" (my personal favorite of the lot) to kick off the album's second half, a tune of nicely layered textures replete with bell-tower chimes and featuring a melodically crafted guitar solo. The best part of all may be the lo-fi fadeout, evoking images straight out of 1922's Nosferatu. From here, Ghost satisfies its more pop-centric listeners with \\"Body and Blood\\" and \\"Idolatrine.\\" These are my least-favorite offerings; your mileage may vary.<br /><br />Infestissumam turns back to the heavier side on \\"Depth of Satan's Eyes\\" and closes well enough with \\"Monstrance Clock,\\" which caps with a satisfying coda built upon layers of guitar, synth and chant.<br /><br />All in all, this release proves to me that Ghost isn't just a flash in the pan. Although I gave it four stars, I can see the rating nudging a bit higher for me in the coming months. Given enough spins, I feel confident most doom rockers will get pulled in just as I did. This is a story of a group of kids who seem to be the only ones who know about and can see an unspeakable evil entity who is killing children in their hometown of Derry Maine.  This entity (who's name is revealed as Pennywise to the kids), terrorizes the community for a number of years until the group of kids seemingly banishes him forever (or so they think).  There's one problem: terrible things start happening again in the town of Derry Maine as these kids have now become adults and have families of their own.  They soon realize that they must regroup, travel back to their old hometown, and fight Pennywise in one last showdown where nothing is certain - especially their own survival.  I absolutely loved this book and to this day it remains in my list of top 5 books of all-time.  I guarantee that if you read it, you will not want to put it down until the end. B00000J9HE|5.0|These recordings dating from the Sixties have throughout the passing decades defined my  and many other people's impression of Sibelius' greatness. None other than the composer in his own words supported  it, when he wrote to Walter Legge that Karajan was the only conductor who (in his estimation) really understood his music.<br />Speaking for myself, they have worn extremely well. Every time I return to audition one of them, the anticipation of a profound immersion into this spiritual landscape is reinforced. There is nothing spectacular here for its own sake here; each symphony has its own soul and its own colouring; there is no trace of routine, but an absolutely honest and at the same deep immersion into this sound world.<br />These DG recordings, also extremely well recorded for their time, have therefore served me as a reliable yardstick for comparing newer releases. I can only report what my experience has been. While several issues have come close to matching them, none has exceeded them in any of the relevant aspects. This includes Karajan's own set for EMI, which is in many respects a letdown after the high expectations, especially for a superior recording technology. But regrettable, the fussy manicuring impulse got the upper hand on that occasion.<br />Accordingly the DG recording holds fast as the upper benchmark.<br />The outcome is something I could hardly state for many recordings, and it will sound paradoxical. But these DG recording have become part of the furniture in my musical imagination, and I mean this in the positive sense that they reflect my own inner responses to Sibelius pretty much to perfection. I can say this of no other recording of this composer's music.<br />If music listening is a form of induced meditation, capable of opening up vistas of our imaginative world such as no other art can, then it might seem that these recordings have transformed over the decades into something not unlike the pictures I have on my walls. All carefully chosen on the criterion that they will \\"speak\\" to me when I ask for it. I can think of no higher praise than this to bestow on these recordings.<br />But I don't expect that you share my point of view.<br />Nevertheless I am not alone in my opinion. These issues have set standards for other critical minds as well; and to allow yourself to be addressed by them on the same level requires nothing more than to set aside the hankering after novelty and diversion. Lasting values are what the word implies: Values that tend to hang around for a long time. They can go out of fashion for a while, but always return, because they transcend fashions. This happens because if a trend is not match by insight, then it will exhaust itself. But insight is the scarcest commodity of all, and it certainly cannot be acquired simply by wishing it or even by studying for it. Insight rests experience.<br />And so five stars to end, because this site does not allow for rosettes. (Maybe just as well, because then it would be full of rosettes!).

B005XBKXV2|5.0|The Lion King, one of the most well-known Disney movies of all time and my childhood generation, begins with the birth of Simba. His dad is King Mufasa, who boldly teaches him about the circle of life. Scar, who is Simba's uncle has a plan to take over the throne, because of his jealousy towards his brother Mufasa who is king. So Scar sets up numerous traps to get Mufasa killed and throw Simba off, since he believes that Simba is too young to become King after Mufasa's planned death. Mufasa is killed when Scar slings him to the bottom of a cliff after trying to rescue Simba who was in a stampede. After his death, Scar convinces Simba that it was his fault for his father's death and the best thing to do is to run far away. He tells the jungle that Simba and Mufasa were both killed, and his is next in line for the throne. Later in life once Simba is a grown man, Nala finds him in the jungle and tells him how everyone thinks he is dead, so she convinces him to return and claim throne. Simba returns and battles Scar, who is defeated by Simba and is finally the King of the Jungle. Simba and Nala are in love, and the movie ends with a newborn cub of theirs. I included the teaching of the circle of life, because this was a prime belief in the Indigenous religions. Mufasa teaches Simba that everything one sees exists together in balance, and explains that everyone is connected. Simba believes that the dead are not separated by the living, when Rafiki tells him to look in the clear water at his reflection and Simba sees his father, Rafiki replies that his father is living in him. They also live among all the land and do not take the water and jungle for granted, like the Indigenous people who practically worship the land, rivers, and sky and made hymns out of this. I grew up watching the Lion King, but after watching it looking for religious connections especially relating to Indigenous religions, it's amazing how much is intertwined and symbolized.

I agree with the record label that the music in this CD 'Let's Face The Music And Dance' is difficult to categorize : it's rather a balanced combination of jazzy, bluesy, popish/rocky, country and latin influenced tracks.<br /><br />Jazzy tracks include the wonderful opening/title track \\"Let's Face The Music And Dance\\" : a smooth jazzy track with a beautiful melody and the unmistaken vocals by the master Willie Nelson & \\"You'll Never Know\\". He would like us to just 'Let's Face The Music And Dance', not try to box him in some category of music or style. B00BKBCF38|3.0|As the title of this review gives away, I felt that the first full length release by this band was an absolute masterpiece. Opus Eponymous came along at a time when most of the music I had been waiting for was letting me down. So many albums that I thought would be monumental were not living up to the expectation, and I really needed to find a winner. Opus was a winner, and I give it a perfect score. There is not a bad song in the bunch on Opus, and I never get tired of any of the songs.  Like another reviewer, I managed to get an advance copy of Infestissumam, and have been listening for a while. First off, I must say that this album is very different than Opus, except for a couple of songs. Both of the early singles for Year Zero, and Secular Haze could have easily been on the first record, but the rest of this album is a departure from that sound. This album has more of a brooding feel. less theatrical, less melodic, and dryer sounding. If the first album was a wild night on the town with a dozen of your friends, this is dinner at home with a couple of close friends. Ghost is great because they are a band that brings a new feel to something you have seen a million times. Kiss, GWAR, Green Jello, etc. etc. have all done the costume gimmick, and managed to put some good music with it, but Ghost is the first one to make the music the real star of the show. If you had only heard the songs, and never seen a picture of the band you would not know that they wear the costumes, and are anonymous musicians.<br /><br />This album does give a very 70's vibe to the listener, but not in an arena rock way. This music reminds me of good rock music that your older brother listened to in the 70's, but nobody knew who they were. The carnival organ sound to me is a nice touch to their sound and adds an evil overtone on otherwise straight forward tunes. As with the first album, you will not find any harsh vocals on this release, but you may find a few whispers here and there.<br /><br />Some people have drawn parallels to Mercyful Fate, Blue Oyster Cult, and Black Sabbath. I don't get either comparison personally. The only similarity I see with Mercyful Fate is that both bands have clean vocals, and to BOC, that they have the 70's vibe that I talked about earlier. I don't think the two bands sound anything alike. That is one of the best things about this band. They don't sound like anyone else currently making music. It's not metal, or pop, or hard rock, or doom, but a combination of all. This is just good song writing, played well, and some of it is pretty memorable. This is not an album that has the \\"guitar solo of the year\\", or \\"wow, listen to that drummer!\\" This album is good songwriting, good musicianship, straightforward rock music with just enough campy-ness to make it fun. They have a satanic message, but it is just all part of the show. This is a band of characters that makes some pretty satisfying tunes. The satanism is just part of the show.<br /><br />A lot of people have been critical of this band, and album for all of the positive reasons I listed above. If you don't like this stuff, you are taking it too seriously. If you criticize the costumes, you're taking it too seriously. If you criticize their playing, you're taking it too seriously. If you criticize the Satanism, you're taking it too seriously. People that are looking for the fastest blast beats, or the most blistering leads, or the most disjointed grindcore, are looking for different music. This is not that kind of band, or experience. This is for having a fun listen every once in a while, and not having to worry about figuring it out. One listen and you will either like it or not. Most people I see that are bashing this are hipsters that are just too cool for Ghost.<br /><br />I do have some criticism about this album that pushes me down to a three star review. First is the sound engineering. I am someone who has a pretty good sound system at home, and a really great onein the car. On a good sound system you can really tell that the mix is too compressed. The drums are too \\"boomy\\" on some tracks, and the guitars get a little lost. For being a larger budget release than Opus Eponymous, it sounds like a rookie was working the sound board on this one. It is not terrible, but it is definitely lacking the production of the first album. Another criticism is the songwriting. On Opus Eponymous the songs flowed into one another, and formed a cohesive collection of tunes. This album seems like it was written in about three sessions that were about a year apart, and I almost think it could have been three separate short releases, instead of one LP. Not as cohesive as the first record. If you are wondering why I reference the first record so much, it is because I would consider it a five star release.<br /><br />Lastly, I got two CD copies of this, one download card, and a copy on vinyl. I have not listened to the download copy, and the CD's have the muddy sound I talked about here. The vinyl is what I was most excited to listen to, and the physical quality of the vinyl is a joke. It is one of the thinnest pieces of vinyl I have ever owned, and out of the sleeve is warped to the point where my turntables cartridge scrapes the record as it spins. I have read other reviews that talk about this too, so it is apparently a common problem. Seriously the quality of the vinyl is one of the worst I have seen. The packaging is good quality, but there is not much to it. No cool booklet, inserts, or any of that. It is simply a record, and a sleeve. That is fine, because it is the music that I want, but I have to say that if you are a vinyl person to stay away from this one. Surprising to say the least. With all of the anticipation over this release, I thought this would have gotten the royal treatment on the vinyl version with heavyweight quality, and extra's that come with a heavyweight price. Simply not the case. Ironically, the sound quality is best on the vinyl, although the physical quality of it is junk.<br /><br />Overall, if you are a Ghost fan you should get this. It is not a terrible release, it just could have been better. Like other reviewers have said, hopefully the third album brings the best of one, and two.

B00B999E12|3.0|The Place Beyond the Pines is a crime drama directed by Derek Cianfrance and starring Ryan Gosling, Bradley Cooper, Eva Mendes, Rose Byrne and Ray Liotta. Set in Schenectady, New York, over a 20-year period, the film is a riveting drama about fathers and sons, and the ramifications that the actions of one generation can have on the next. Gosling stars as motorcycle daredevil Luke Glanton, who turns to a life of crime robbing banks in order to provide for his baby son, Jason, and the mother, Romina (Mendes), who accepts Luke's help only reluctantly. Luke's increasing desperation brings him into contact with Avery Cross (Cooper), a Schenectady cop with a family history of running for political office, who is dealing with a shaky marriage to his wife Jennifer (Byrne) and a young son named AJ, pressure from his own father, and his own discovery of corruption among his colleagues. The film takes some unexpected twists and turns in its second half - which I won't reveal here - suffice to say that Cianfrance's measured direction and languid pacing allows the film to develop into a slow-burning familial drama that is both hypnotic and engrossing, especially as the true depth of the generational secrets are revealed.<br /><br />The music for The Place Beyond the Pines is by composer Mike Patton, who is still best known in the music world as the lead singer of the alternative metal band Faith No More in the 1990s, but whose musical experimentations have taken him to writing, producing and performing as a multi-instrumentalist across numerous genres and with dozens of collaborators. In the film music world, Patton made his debut writing music for the action movie sequel Crank: High Voltage in 2009, and he also wrote the score for the Italian-language drama La Solitudine dei Numeri Primi in 2010. The Place Beyond the Pines is his third feature film, and if the strength of this score is anything to go by, he may well have added another significant string to his already tightly-pulled musical bow.<br /><br />But first, a word of warning to \\"conventional\\" score fans; The Place Beyond the Pines is not a conventional score. Patton's music is a combination of electronic tones, electric and acoustic guitar accents, and a sampled choir - as far as I can tell there are no live orchestral instrumental in play in the score at all. If you dislike any score music that doesn't make use of a traditional orchestral complement, give this one a miss, because it won't appeal to your sensibilities at all. For those whose tastes extend into the more unusual, however, The Place Beyond the Pines is worth exploring, as Patton certainly manages to do some interesting things with such a self-limiting palette.<br /><br />Thematically, there is no real recurring elements which can be pointed to as a \\"main theme\\" or recurring melodic identity; the cue \\"The Snow Angel\\", which features a lonely-sounding piano motif and languid accompanying chords plays just once on the soundtrack, but features prominently in the film's trailer and re-appears several times in the film itself, usually as an accompaniment to a tracking shot following a forlorn bike rider winding his way through a lonely road thickly lines with trees.<br /><br />Instead, Patton is more concerned with textures and moods, creating a sense of loneliness and general despondency through the music which matches the pervading tone of the film; in The Place Beyond the Pines, no-one is ever really happy, and the past weighs down heavily on everyone. Patton's score drifts hypnotically from one set piece to the next, alternating between light and dark, adding layers of gloom to the increasingly desperate central characters.<br /><br />The highlights are certainly the cues where the chorus comes into play - the oppressively pretty opening track \\"Schenectady\\", for example, more than insinuates that something is not right in the city for which the film is named, before moving on through the combination of voices and twinkling guitars of \\"Beyond the Pines\\" and the unusual \\"Coniferae\\", which has a slightly skewed electric guitar riff and other processed effects. The conclusive \\"Handsome Luke\\" is a little more disturbing than most of its predecessors, making use of hard-edged industrial textures, a bigger percussion section, and brutally explosive, almost Goldenthal-esque sound design elements to capture the intensity of the film's finale.<br /><br />Several songs and classical pieces round off Milan's album presentation. The dreamy rendition of Burt Bacharach's \\"Please Stay\\" by Liverpudlian band The Cryin' Shames from 1961 matches to mood of the rest of the score. The new recording of Italian composer Gregorio Allegri's 17th century liturgical chant \\"Miserere Mei\\" by Vladimir Ivanoff and the Osnabrck Youth Choir is haunting and moving, as is the spellbinding performance of Estonian composer Arvo Prt's minimalist 1977 classical composition \\"Fratres\\", reminding the listener of why it has inspired similar textural experiments by film composers ranging from James Horner to James Newton Howard. There's even an equally mesmerizing cue from Ennio Morricone's 1969 score for the film Cuore di Mamma entitled \\"Ninna Nanna Per Adulteri\\", before the roughneck tones of indie folk rock band Bon Iver bring the album to a close with \\"The Wolves - Act I and II\\".<br /><br />I can't quite explain why I enjoyed the score for The Place Beyond the Pines as much as I did, because it's not really the type of music I usually go for. It might be something to do with the way it gives the film itself an appropriately overwhelming mood of impending tragedy, or Patton's intelligent way of arranging his collages of sounds into fascinating musical effects. Whatever the reason is, I found The Place Beyond the Pines to be an oddly affecting, unusually fascinating score which veers significantly from the mainstream.
